<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Wave</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Repeated oscillation around equilibrium</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">This article is about waves in the scientific sense. For waves on seas and lakes, see <a href="/wiki/Wind_wave" title="Wind wave">Wind wave</a>. For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Wave_(disambiguation)" title="Wave (disambiguation)">Wave (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1097763485">.mw-parser-output .ambox{border:1px solid #a2a9b1;border-left:10px solid #36c;background-color:#fbfbfb;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div class="mbox-image-div"><img alt="" data-file-height="20" data-file-width="50" decoding="async" height="20" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Mergefrom.svg/50px-Mergefrom.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Mergefrom.svg/75px-Mergefrom.svg.png 1.5x, (<a href="/wiki/Talk:Wave#Proposed_merge_of_Wave_propagation_into_wave" title="Talk:Wave">Discuss</a>)<small><i> Proposed since May 2022.</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:2006-01-14_Surface_waves.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1320" data-file-width="1920" decoding="async" height="151" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/2006-01-14_Surface_waves.jpg/220px-2006-01-14_Surface_waves.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/2006-01-14_Surface_waves.jpg/330px-2006-01-14_Surface_waves.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/2006-01-14_Surface_waves.jpg/440px-2006-01-14_Surface_waves.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:2006-01-14_Surface_waves.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Surface waves in water showing water ripples</div></div></div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Physics" title="Physics">physics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">mathematics</a>, and related fields, a <b>wave</b> is a propagating dynamic disturbance (change from <a href="/wiki/List_of_types_of_equilibrium" title="List of types of equilibrium">equilibrium</a>) of one or more <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quantities" title="Quantities">quantities</a>. Waves can be <a href="/wiki/Periodic_function" title="Periodic function">periodic</a>, in which case those quantities oscillate repeatedly about an equilibrium (resting) value at some <a href="/wiki/Frequency" title="Frequency">frequency</a>. When the entire <a href="/wiki/Waveform" title="Waveform">waveform</a> moves in one direction, it is said to be a <i>traveling wave</i>; by contrast, a pair of <a href="/wiki/Superposition_principle" title="Superposition principle">superimposed</a> periodic waves traveling in opposite directions makes a <i><a href="/wiki/Standing_wave" title="Standing wave">standing wave</a></i>. In a standing wave, the amplitude of vibration has nulls at some positions where the wave amplitude appears smaller or even zero. Waves are often described by a <i><a href="/wiki/Wave_equation" title="Wave equation">wave equation</a></i> (standing wave field of two opposite waves) or a <a href="/wiki/One-way_wave_equation" title="One-way wave equation">one-way wave equation</a> for single wave propagation in a defined direction. 
</p><p>Two types of waves are most commonly studied in <a href="/wiki/Classical_physics" title="Classical physics">classical physics</a>. In a <i><a href="/wiki/Mechanical_wave" title="Mechanical wave">mechanical wave</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Stress_(mechanics)" title="Stress (mechanics)">stress</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Strain_(mechanics)" title="Strain (mechanics)">strain</a> fields oscillate about a mechanical equilibrium. A mechanical wave is a local <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deformation_(mechanics)" title="Deformation (mechanics)">deformation (strain)</a> in some physical medium that propagates from particle to particle by creating local <a href="/wiki/Stress_(mechanics)" title="Stress (mechanics)">stresses</a> that cause strain in neighboring particles too. For example, <a href="/wiki/Sound" title="Sound">sound</a> waves are variations of the local <a href="/wiki/Sound_pressure" title="Sound pressure">pressure</a> and <a href="/wiki/Particle_velocity" title="Particle velocity">particle motion</a> that propagate through the medium. Other examples of mechanical waves are <a href="/wiki/Seismic_wave" title="Seismic wave">seismic waves</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gravity_wave" title="Gravity wave">gravity waves</a>, <a href="/wiki/Surface_wave" title="Surface wave">surface waves</a>, <a href="/wiki/String_vibration" title="String vibration">string vibrations</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Vortex" title="Vortex">vortices</a>. In an <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_wave" title="Electromagnetic wave">electromagnetic wave</a></i> (such as light), coupling between the electric and magnetic fields which sustains propagation of a wave involving these fields according to <a href="/wiki/Maxwell%27s_equations" title="Maxwell's equations">Maxwell's equations</a>. Electromagnetic waves can travel through a <a href="/wiki/Vacuum" title="Vacuum">vacuum</a> and through some <a href="/wiki/Dielectric" title="Dielectric">dielectric</a> media (at wavelengths where they are considered <a href="/wiki/Transparency_and_translucency" title="Transparency and translucency">transparent</a>). Electromagnetic waves, according to their frequencies (or <a href="/wiki/Wavelength" title="Wavelength">wavelengths</a>) have more specific designations including <a href="/wiki/Radio_wave" title="Radio wave">radio waves</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Infrared_radiation" title="Infrared radiation">infrared radiation</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Terahertz_waves" title="Terahertz waves">terahertz waves</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Visible_light" title="Visible light">visible light</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ultraviolet_radiation" title="Ultraviolet radiation">ultraviolet radiation</a>, <a href="/wiki/X-ray" title="X-ray">X-rays</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gamma_ray" title="Gamma ray">gamma rays</a>.
</p><p>Other types of waves include <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_wave" title="Gravitational wave">gravitational waves</a>, which are disturbances in <a href="/wiki/Spacetime" title="Spacetime">spacetime</a> that propagate according to <a href="/wiki/General_relativity" title="General relativity">general relativity</a>; <a href="/wiki/Heat_equation" title="Heat equation">heat diffusion waves</a>; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plasma_wave" title="Plasma wave">plasma waves</a> that combine mechanical deformations and electromagnetic fields; <a href="/wiki/Reaction%E2%80%93diffusion_system" title="Reaction–diffusion system">reaction–diffusion waves</a>, such as in the <a href="/wiki/Belousov%E2%80%93Zhabotinsky_reaction" title="Belousov–Zhabotinsky reaction">Belousov–Zhabotinsky reaction</a>; and many more. Mechanical and electromagnetic waves transfer <a href="/wiki/Energy" title="Energy">energy</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Momentum_(physics)" title="Momentum (physics)">momentum</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Information" title="Information">information</a>, but they do not transfer particles in the medium. In mathematics and <a href="/wiki/Electronics" title="Electronics">electronics</a> waves are studied as <a href="/wiki/Signal" title="Signal">signals</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> On the other hand, some waves have <a href="/wiki/Envelope_(waves)" title="Envelope (waves)">envelopes</a> which do not move at all such as <a href="/wiki/Standing_wave" title="Standing wave">standing waves</a> (which are fundamental to music) and <a href="/wiki/Hydraulic_jump" title="Hydraulic jump">hydraulic jumps</a>. Some, like the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Probability_wave" title="Probability wave">probability waves</a> of <a href="/wiki/Quantum_mechanics" title="Quantum mechanics">quantum mechanics</a>, may be completely static<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Accuracy_dispute#Disputed_statement" title="Wikipedia:Accuracy dispute"><span title="The material near this tag is possibly inaccurate or nonfactual. (August 2020)">dubious</span></a> <span class="metadata"> – <a href="/wiki/Talk:Wave#Dubious" title="Talk:Wave">discuss</a></span></i>]</sup>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Santos_E_et_al_Neuroimage_2014_.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="308" data-file-width="610" decoding="async" height="111" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Santos_E_et_al_Neuroimage_2014_.gif/220px-Santos_E_et_al_Neuroimage_2014_.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Santos_E_et_al_Neuroimage_2014_.gif/330px-Santos_E_et_al_Neuroimage_2014_.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Santos_E_et_al_Neuroimage_2014_.gif/440px-Santos_E_et_al_Neuroimage_2014_.gif 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Santos_E_et_al_Neuroimage_2014_.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Example of biological waves expanding over the brain cortex, an example of <a href="/wiki/Cortical_spreading_depression" title="Cortical spreading depression">spreading depolarizations</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>A physical wave <a href="/wiki/Field_(physics)" title="Field (physics)">field</a> is almost always confined to some finite region of space, called its <i>domain</i>. For example, the seismic waves generated by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Earthquakes" title="Earthquakes">earthquakes</a> are significant only in the interior and surface of the planet, so they can be ignored outside it. However, waves with infinite domain, that extend over the whole space, are commonly studied in mathematics, and are very valuable tools for understanding physical waves in finite domains.
</p><p>A <i><a href="/wiki/Plane_wave" title="Plane wave">plane wave</a></i> is an important mathematical idealization where the disturbance is identical along any (infinite) plane <a href="/wiki/Normal_(geometry)" title="Normal (geometry)">normal</a> to a specific direction of travel. Mathematically, the simplest wave is a <i><a href="/wiki/Sinusoidal_plane_wave" title="Sinusoidal plane wave">sinusoidal plane wave</a></i> in which at any point the field experiences <a href="/wiki/Simple_harmonic_motion" title="Simple harmonic motion">simple harmonic motion</a> at one frequency. In linear media, complicated waves can generally be decomposed as the sum of many sinusoidal plane waves having <a href="/wiki/Angular_spectrum_method" title="Angular spectrum method">different directions of propagation</a> and/or <a href="/wiki/Fourier_transform" title="Fourier transform">different frequencies</a>. A plane wave is classified as a <i><a href="/wiki/Transverse_wave" title="Transverse wave">transverse wave</a></i> if the field disturbance at each point is described by a vector perpendicular to the direction of propagation (also the direction of energy transfer); or <i><a href="/wiki/Longitudinal_wave" title="Longitudinal wave">longitudinal wave</a></i> if those vectors are aligned with the propagation direction. Mechanical waves include both transverse and longitudinal waves; on the other hand electromagnetic plane waves are strictly transverse while sound waves in fluids (such as air) can only be longitudinal. That physical direction of an oscillating field relative to the propagation direction is also referred to as the wave's <i><a href="/wiki/Polarization_(waves)" title="Polarization (waves)">polarization</a></i>, which can be an important attribute.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Mathematical_description">Mathematical description</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wave&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Mathematical description">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Single_waves">Single waves</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wave&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Single waves">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A wave can be described just like a field, namely as a <a href="/wiki/Function_(mathematics)" title="Function (mathematics)">function</a> <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle F(x,t)}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>F</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mi>x</mi>
<mo>,</mo>
<mi>t</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle F(x,t)}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle F(x,t)}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/1e09b52b64302d62ab2c63538a7d512ffb3a16fe" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:6.753ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span> where <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle x}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>x</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle x}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="x" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/87f9e315fd7e2ba406057a97300593c4802b53e4" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.33ex; height:1.676ex;"/></span> is a position and <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle t}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>t</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle t}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="t" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/65658b7b223af9e1acc877d848888ecdb4466560" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:0.84ex; height:2.009ex;"/></span> is a time.
</p><p>The value of <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle x}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>x</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle x}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="x" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/87f9e315fd7e2ba406057a97300593c4802b53e4" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.33ex; height:1.676ex;"/></span> is a point of space, specifically in the region where the wave is defined. In mathematical terms, it is usually a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vector_(mathematics)" title="Vector (mathematics)">vector</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Analytic_geometry" title="Analytic geometry">Cartesian three-dimensional space</a> <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \mathbb {R} ^{3}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<msup>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi mathvariant="double-struck">R</mi>
</mrow>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>3</mn>
</mrow>
</msup>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \mathbb {R} ^{3}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\mathbb {R} ^{3}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f936ddf584f8f3dd2a0ed08917001b7a404c10b5" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:2.732ex; height:2.676ex;"/></span>. However, in many cases one can ignore one dimension, and let <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle x}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>x</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle x}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="x" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/87f9e315fd7e2ba406057a97300593c4802b53e4" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.33ex; height:1.676ex;"/></span> be a point of the Cartesian plane <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \mathbb {R} ^{2}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<msup>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi mathvariant="double-struck">R</mi>
</mrow>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>2</mn>
</mrow>
</msup>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \mathbb {R} ^{2}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\mathbb {R} ^{2}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/e150115ab9f63023215109595b76686a1ff890fd" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:2.732ex; height:2.676ex;"/></span>. This is the case, for example, when studying vibrations of a drum skin. One may even restrict <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle x}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>x</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle x}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="x" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/87f9e315fd7e2ba406057a97300593c4802b53e4" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.33ex; height:1.676ex;"/></span> to a point of the Cartesian line <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \mathbb {R} }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi mathvariant="double-struck">R</mi>
</mrow>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \mathbb {R} }</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\mathbb {R} " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/786849c765da7a84dbc3cce43e96aad58a5868dc" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.678ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> — that is, the set of <a href="/wiki/Real_number" title="Real number">real numbers</a>. This is the case, for example, when studying vibrations in a <a href="/wiki/String_(music)" title="String (music)">violin string</a> or <a href="/wiki/Recorder_(musical_instrument)" title="Recorder (musical instrument)">recorder</a>. The time <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle t}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>t</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle t}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="t" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/65658b7b223af9e1acc877d848888ecdb4466560" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:0.84ex; height:2.009ex;"/></span>, on the other hand, is always assumed to be a <a href="/wiki/Scalar_(physics)" title="Scalar (physics)">scalar</a>; that is, a real number.
</p><p>The value of <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle F(x,t)}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>F</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mi>x</mi>
<mo>,</mo>
<mi>t</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle F(x,t)}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle F(x,t)}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/1e09b52b64302d62ab2c63538a7d512ffb3a16fe" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:6.753ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span> can be any physical quantity of interest assigned to the point <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle x}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>x</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle x}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="x" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/87f9e315fd7e2ba406057a97300593c4802b53e4" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.33ex; height:1.676ex;"/></span> that may vary with time. For example, if <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle F}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>F</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle F}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="F" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/545fd099af8541605f7ee55f08225526be88ce57" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.741ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> represents the vibrations inside an elastic solid, the value of <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle F(x,t)}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>F</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mi>x</mi>
<mo>,</mo>
<mi>t</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle F(x,t)}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle F(x,t)}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/1e09b52b64302d62ab2c63538a7d512ffb3a16fe" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:6.753ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span> is usually a vector that gives the current displacement from <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle x}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>x</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle x}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="x" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/87f9e315fd7e2ba406057a97300593c4802b53e4" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.33ex; height:1.676ex;"/></span> of the material particles that would be at the point <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle x}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>x</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle x}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="x" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/87f9e315fd7e2ba406057a97300593c4802b53e4" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.33ex; height:1.676ex;"/></span> in the absence of vibration. For an electromagnetic wave, the value of <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle F}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>F</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle F}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="F" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/545fd099af8541605f7ee55f08225526be88ce57" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.741ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> can be the <a href="/wiki/Electric_field" title="Electric field">electric field</a> vector <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle E}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>E</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle E}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="E" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/4232c9de2ee3eec0a9c0a19b15ab92daa6223f9b" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.776ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span>, or the <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_field" title="Magnetic field">magnetic field</a> vector <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle H}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>H</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle H}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="H" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/75a9edddcca2f782014371f75dca39d7e13a9c1b" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:2.064ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span>, or any related quantity, such as the <a href="/wiki/Poynting_vector" title="Poynting vector">Poynting vector</a> <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle E\times H}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>E</mi>
<mo>×<!-- × --></mo>
<mi>H</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle E\times H}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle E\times H}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/01c943e4f3c286466844d3f602cf99a9eb39b419" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:6.68ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span>. <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Wave_families">Wave families</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wave&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Wave families">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Sometimes one is interested in a single specific wave. More often, however, one needs to understand large set of possible waves; like all the ways that a drum skin can vibrate after being struck once with a <a href="/wiki/Drum_stick" title="Drum stick">drum stick</a>, or all the possible <a href="/wiki/Radar" title="Radar">radar</a> echos one could get from an <a href="/wiki/Airplane" title="Airplane">airplane</a> that may be approaching an <a href="/wiki/Airport" title="Airport">airport</a>.
</p><p>In some of those situations, one may describe such a family of waves by a function <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle F(A,B,\ldots ;x,t)}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>F</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mi>A</mi>
<mo>,</mo>
<mi>B</mi>
<mo>,</mo>
<mo>…<!-- … --></mo>
<mo>;</mo>
<mi>x</mi>
<mo>,</mo>
<mi>t</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle F(A,B,\ldots ;x,t)}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle F(A,B,\ldots ;x,t)}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/71569f06072180afdea6ab460bb74bf7c12017a6" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:16.473ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span> that depends on certain <a href="/wiki/Parameter" title="Parameter">parameters</a> <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle A,B,\ldots }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

</mrow>

</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle A,B,\ldots }" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/58f60b534d9a4d494d9a54a1d16df4dacbef2b98" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:8.298ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span>, besides <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle x}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</semantics>

Then one can obtain different waves — that is, different functions of <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle x}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Half-open_pipe_wave.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="250" data-file-width="663" decoding="async" height="83" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Half-open_pipe_wave.gif/220px-Half-open_pipe_wave.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Half-open_pipe_wave.gif/330px-Half-open_pipe_wave.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Half-open_pipe_wave.gif/440px-Half-open_pipe_wave.gif 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Half-open_pipe_wave.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sound pressure standing wave in a half-open pipe playing the 7th harmonic of the fundamental (<i>n</i> = 4)</div></div></div>
<p>For example, the sound pressure inside a <a href="/wiki/Recorder_(musical_instrument)" title="Recorder (musical instrument)">recorder</a> that is playing a "pure" note is typically a <a href="/wiki/Standing_wave" title="Standing wave">standing wave</a>, that can be written as
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle F(A,L,n,c;x,t)=A\left(\cos 2\pi x{\frac {2n-1}{4L}}\right)\left(\cos 2\pi ct{\frac {2n-1}{4L}}\right)}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">

<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mi>A</mi>
<mo>,</mo>
<mi>L</mi>
<mo>,</mo>
<mi>n</mi>
<mo>,</mo>
<mi>c</mi>
<mo>;</mo>
<mi>x</mi>
<mo>,</mo>
<mi>t</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
<mo>=</mo>
<mi>A</mi>
<mrow>
<mo>(</mo>
<mrow>
<mi>cos</mi>
<mo>⁡<!-- ⁡ --></mo>
<mn>2</mn>

<p>The parameter <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle A}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>

</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle A}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="A" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/7daff47fa58cdfd29dc333def748ff5fa4c923e3" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.743ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> defines the amplitude of the wave (that is, the maximum sound pressure in the bore, which is related to the loudness of the note); <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle c}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="c" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/86a67b81c2de995bd608d5b2df50cd8cd7d92455" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.007ex; height:1.676ex;"/></span> is the speed of sound; <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle L}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

(The position <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle x}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

The quantity <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \lambda =4L/(2n-1)}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

Many general properties of these waves can be inferred from this general equation, without choosing specific values for the parameters.
</p><p>As another example, it may be that the vibrations of a drum skin after a single strike depend only on the distance <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle r}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>r</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle r}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="r" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/0d1ecb613aa2984f0576f70f86650b7c2a132538" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.049ex; height:1.676ex;"/></span> from the center of the skin to the strike point, and on the strength <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle s}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>s</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle s}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="s" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/01d131dfd7673938b947072a13a9744fe997e632" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.09ex; height:1.676ex;"/></span> of the strike. Then the vibration for all possible strikes can be described by a function <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle F(r,s;x,t)}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>

</p><p>Sometimes the family of waves of interest has infinitely many parameters. For example, one may want to describe what happens to the temperature in a metal bar when it is initially heated at various temperatures at different points along its length, and then allowed to cool by itself in vacuum. In that case, instead of a scalar or vector, the parameter would have to be a function <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle h}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

<semantics>

Then the temperatures at later times can be expressed by a function <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle F}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Differential_wave_equations">Differential wave equations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wave&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Differential wave equations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Another way to describe and study a family of waves is to give a mathematical equation that, instead of explicitly giving the value of <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle F(x,t)}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>F</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mi>x</mi>
<mo>,</mo>
<mi>t</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle F(x,t)}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle F(x,t)}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/1e09b52b64302d62ab2c63538a7d512ffb3a16fe" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:6.753ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span>, only constrains how those values can change with time. Then the family of waves in question consists of all functions <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle F}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>F</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle F}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="F" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/545fd099af8541605f7ee55f08225526be88ce57" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.741ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> that satisfy those constraints — that is, all <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solution_(mathematics)" title="Solution (mathematics)">solutions</a> of the equation.
</p><p>This approach is extremely important in physics, because the constraints usually are a consequence of the physical processes that cause the wave to evolve. For example, if <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle F(x,t)}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>F</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mi>x</mi>
<mo>,</mo>
<mi>t</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle F(x,t)}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle F(x,t)}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/1e09b52b64302d62ab2c63538a7d512ffb3a16fe" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:6.753ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span> is the temperature inside a block of some <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Homogeneous" title="Homogeneous">homogeneous</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Isotropic" title="Isotropic">isotropic</a> solid material, its evolution is constrained by the <a href="/wiki/Partial_differential_equation" title="Partial differential equation">partial differential equation</a>
</p>

</p><p>This equation can be derived from the laws of physics that govern the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heat_diffusion" title="Heat diffusion">diffusion of heat</a> in solid media. For that reason, it is called the <a href="/wiki/Heat_equation" title="Heat equation">heat equation</a> in mathematics, even though it applies to many other physical quantities besides temperatures.
</p><p>For another example, we can describe all possible sounds echoing within a container of gas by a function <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle F(x,t)}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle x}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="x" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/87f9e315fd7e2ba406057a97300593c4802b53e4" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.33ex; height:1.676ex;"/></span> by some external process, such as a <a href="/wiki/Loudspeaker" title="Loudspeaker">loudspeaker</a> or <a href="/wiki/Piston" title="Piston">piston</a> right next to <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle p}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>p</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle p}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="p" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/81eac1e205430d1f40810df36a0edffdc367af36" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; margin-left: -0.089ex; width:1.259ex; height:2.009ex;"/></span>.
</p><p>This same differential equation describes the behavior of mechanical vibrations and electromagnetic fields in a homogeneous isotropic non-conducting solid. Note that this equation differs from that of heat flow only in that the left-hand side is <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \partial ^{2}F/\partial t^{2}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</mrow>

</math></span><img alt="\partial F/\partial t" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/8329bd506a9a29a28c423fe18b7036052a6846fe" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:6.379ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span>. Yet this small change makes a huge difference on the set of solutions <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle F}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

This differential equation is called "the" <a href="/wiki/Wave_equation" title="Wave equation">wave equation</a> in mathematics, even though it describes only one very special kind of waves.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Wave_in_elastic_medium">Wave in elastic medium</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wave&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Wave in elastic medium">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Wave_equation" title="Wave equation">Wave equation</a> and <a href="/wiki/D%27Alembert%27s_formula" title="D'Alembert's formula">D'Alembert's formula</a></div>
<p>Consider a traveling <a href="/wiki/Transverse_wave" title="Transverse wave">transverse wave</a> (which may be a <a href="/wiki/Pulse_(physics)" title="Pulse (physics)">pulse</a>) on a string (the medium). Consider the string to have a single spatial dimension. Consider this wave as traveling
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nonsinusoidal_wavelength.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="287" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="112" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Nonsinusoidal_wavelength.svg/200px-Nonsinusoidal_wavelength.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Nonsinusoidal_wavelength.svg/300px-Nonsinusoidal_wavelength.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Nonsinusoidal_wavelength.svg/400px-Nonsinusoidal_wavelength.svg.png 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nonsinusoidal_wavelength.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Wavelength <i>λ</i>, can be measured between any two corresponding points on a waveform</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Superpositionprinciple.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="443" data-file-width="854" decoding="async" height="114" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Superpositionprinciple.gif/220px-Superpositionprinciple.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Superpositionprinciple.gif/330px-Superpositionprinciple.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Superpositionprinciple.gif/440px-Superpositionprinciple.gif 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Superpositionprinciple.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Animation of two waves, the green wave moves to the right while blue wave moves to the left, the net red wave amplitude at each point is the sum of the amplitudes of the individual waves. Note that f(x,t) + g(x,t) = u(x,t)</div></div></div>
<ul><li>in the <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle x}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

For example, let the positive <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle x}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\frac {1}{v^{2}}}{\frac {\partial ^{2}u}{\partial t^{2}}}={\frac {\partial ^{2}u}{\partial x^{2}}}.}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle {\frac {1}{v^{2}}}{\frac {\partial ^{2}u}{\partial t^{2}}}={\frac {\partial ^{2}u}{\partial x^{2}}}.}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/01467652b3a36701ab64ef2902b6e459d04dae16" style="vertical-align: -2.171ex; width:15.89ex; height:6.009ex;"/></span></dd></dl>
<p>General solutions are based upon <a href="/wiki/Duhamel%27s_principle" title="Duhamel's principle">Duhamel's principle</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Struwe_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Struwe-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p><p>Beside the second order wave equations that are describing a standing wave field, the <a href="/wiki/One-way_wave_equation" title="One-way wave equation">one-way wave equation</a> describes the propagation of single wave in a defined direction.  
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Wave_forms">Wave forms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wave&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Wave forms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Waveform" title="Waveform">Waveform</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Waveforms.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="530" data-file-width="620" decoding="async" height="239" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Waveforms.svg/280px-Waveforms.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Waveforms.svg/420px-Waveforms.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Waveforms.svg/560px-Waveforms.svg.png 2x" width="280"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Waveforms.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Sine_wave" title="Sine wave">Sine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Square_wave" title="Square wave">square</a>, <a href="/wiki/Triangle_wave" title="Triangle wave">triangle</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sawtooth_wave" title="Sawtooth wave">sawtooth</a> waveforms.</div></div></div>
<p>The form or shape of <i>F</i> in <a href="/wiki/D%27Alembert%27s_formula" title="D'Alembert's formula">d'Alembert's formula</a> involves the argument <i>x</i> − <i>vt</i>. Constant values of this argument correspond to constant values of <i>F</i>, and these constant values occur if <i>x</i> increases at the same rate that <i>vt</i> increases. That is, the wave shaped like the function <i>F</i> will move in the positive <i>x</i>-direction at velocity <i>v</i> (and <i>G</i> will propagate at the same speed in the negative <i>x</i>-direction).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lyons_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lyons-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the case of a periodic function <i>F</i> with period <i>λ</i>, that is, <i>F</i>(<i>x</i> + <i>λ</i> − <i>vt</i>) = <i>F</i>(<i>x</i> − <i>vt</i>), the periodicity of <i>F</i> in space means that a snapshot of the wave at a given time <i>t</i> finds the wave varying periodically in space with period <i>λ</i> (the <a href="/wiki/Wavelength" title="Wavelength">wavelength</a> of the wave). In a similar fashion, this periodicity of <i>F</i> implies a periodicity in time as well: <i>F</i>(<i>x</i> − <i>v</i>(<i>t</i> + <i>T</i>)) = <i>F</i>(<i>x</i> − <i>vt</i>) provided <i>vT</i> = <i>λ</i>, so an observation of the wave at a fixed location <i>x</i> finds the wave undulating periodically in time with period <i>T = λ</i>/<i>v</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McPherson0_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-McPherson0-10">[10]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Amplitude_and_modulation">Amplitude and modulation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wave&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Amplitude and modulation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Amplitudemodulation.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="443" data-file-width="854" decoding="async" height="114" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Amplitudemodulation.gif/220px-Amplitudemodulation.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Amplitudemodulation.gif/330px-Amplitudemodulation.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Amplitudemodulation.gif/440px-Amplitudemodulation.gif 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Amplitudemodulation.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Amplitude modulation can be achieved through <i>f</i>(<i>x</i>,<i>t</i>) = 1.00×sin(2π/0.10×(<i>x</i>−1.00×<i>t</i>)) and <i>g</i>(<i>x</i>,<i>t</i>) = 1.00×sin(2π/0.11×(<i>x</i>−1.00×<i>t</i>))only the resultant is visible to improve clarity of waveform.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wave_packet.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3964" data-file-width="5478" decoding="async" height="159" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Wave_packet.svg/220px-Wave_packet.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Wave_packet.svg/330px-Wave_packet.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Wave_packet.svg/440px-Wave_packet.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wave_packet.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Illustration of the <i>envelope</i> (the slowly varying red curve) of an amplitude-modulated wave. The fast varying blue curve is the <i>carrier</i> wave, which is being modulated.</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Amplitude_modulation" title="Amplitude modulation">Amplitude modulation</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Frequency_modulation" title="Frequency modulation">Frequency modulation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Phase_modulation" title="Phase modulation">Phase modulation</a></div>
<p>The amplitude of a wave may be constant (in which case the wave is a <i>c.w.</i> or <i><a href="/wiki/Continuous_wave" title="Continuous wave">continuous wave</a></i>), or may be <i>modulated</i> so as to vary with time and/or position. The outline of the variation in amplitude is called the <i>envelope</i> of the wave. Mathematically, the <a href="/wiki/Amplitude_modulation" title="Amplitude modulation">modulated wave</a> can be written in the form:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jirauschek_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jirauschek-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kneubühl_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kneubühl-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lundstrom_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lundstrom-13">[13]</a></sup>
</p>

<p>showing that the envelope moves with the group velocity and retains its shape. <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Phase_velocity_and_group_velocity">Phase velocity and group velocity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wave&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Phase velocity and group velocity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Phase_velocity" title="Phase velocity">Phase velocity</a> and <a href="/wiki/Group_velocity" title="Group velocity">Group velocity</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Envelope_(waves)#Phase_and_group_velocity" title="Envelope (waves)">Envelope (waves) § Phase and group velocity</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wave_group.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="36" data-file-width="465" decoding="async" height="17" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Wave_group.gif/220px-Wave_group.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Wave_group.gif/330px-Wave_group.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Wave_group.gif/440px-Wave_group.gif 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wave_group.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The red square moves with the <a href="/wiki/Phase_velocity" title="Phase velocity">phase velocity</a>, while the green circles propagate with the <a href="/wiki/Group_velocity" title="Group velocity">group velocity</a></div></div></div>
<p>There are two velocities that are associated with waves, the <a href="/wiki/Phase_velocity" title="Phase velocity">phase velocity</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Group_velocity" title="Group velocity">group velocity</a>.
</p><p>Phase velocity is the rate at which the <a href="/wiki/Phase_(waves)" title="Phase (waves)">phase</a> of the wave <a href="/wiki/Wave_propagation" title="Wave propagation">propagates in space</a>: any given phase of the wave (for example, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crest_(physics)" title="Crest (physics)">crest</a>) will appear to travel at the phase velocity. The phase velocity is given in terms of the <a href="/wiki/Wavelength" title="Wavelength">wavelength</a> <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">λ</span> (lambda) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wave_period" title="Wave period">period</a> <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">T</span> as
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle v_{\mathrm {p} }={\frac {\lambda }{T}}.}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<msub>
<mi>v</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi mathvariant="normal">p</mi>
</mrow>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mo>=</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mfrac>
<mi>λ<!-- λ --></mi>
<mi>T</mi>
</mfrac>
</mrow>
<mo>.</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle v_{\mathrm {p} }={\frac {\lambda }{T}}.}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="v_{\mathrm {p} }={\frac {\lambda }{T}}." aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/802ae0d055059b1be7449bd2f85dc1099dd20462" style="vertical-align: -1.838ex; width:8.492ex; height:5.343ex;"/></span></dd></dl>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wave_opposite-group-phase-velocity.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="154" data-file-width="250" decoding="async" height="136" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Wave_opposite-group-phase-velocity.gif/220px-Wave_opposite-group-phase-velocity.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c7/Wave_opposite-group-phase-velocity.gif 1.5x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wave_opposite-group-phase-velocity.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A wave with the group and phase velocities going in different directions</div></div></div>
<p>Group velocity is a property of waves that have a defined envelope, measuring propagation through space (that is, phase velocity) of the overall shape of the waves' amplitudes – modulation or envelope of the wave.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Special_waves">Special waves</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wave&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Special waves">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sine_waves">Sine waves</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wave&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Sine waves">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="excerpt-block"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1066933788">.mw-parser-output .excerpt-hat .mw-editsection-like{font-style:normal}</style><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable dablink excerpt-hat selfref" role="note">This section is an excerpt from <a href="/wiki/Sine_wave" title="Sine wave">Sine wave</a>.<span class="mw-editsection-like plainlinks"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Sine_wave&amp;action=edit">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div><div class="excerpt">
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sine_and_Cosine.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="327" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="255" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Sine_and_Cosine.svg/400px-Sine_and_Cosine.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Sine_and_Cosine.svg/600px-Sine_and_Cosine.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Sine_and_Cosine.svg/800px-Sine_and_Cosine.svg.png 2x" width="400"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sine_and_Cosine.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The graphs of the sine (solid red) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cosine" title="Cosine">cosine</a> (dotted blue) functions are sinusoids of different phases</div></div></div>
A <a href="/wiki/Sine_wave" title="Sine wave">sine wave</a>, sinusoidal wave, or just sinusoid is a <a href="/wiki/Curve" title="Curve">mathematical curve</a> defined in terms of the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sine" title="Sine">sine</a></i> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trigonometric_function" title="Trigonometric function">trigonometric function</a>, of which it is the <a href="/wiki/Graph_of_a_function" title="Graph of a function">graph</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> It is a type of <a href="/wiki/Continuous_wave" title="Continuous wave">continuous wave</a> and also a <a href="/wiki/Smoothness" title="Smoothness">smooth</a> <a href="/wiki/Periodic_function" title="Periodic function">periodic function</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> It occurs often in <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">mathematics</a>, as well as in <a href="/wiki/Physics" title="Physics">physics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Engineering" title="Engineering">engineering</a>, <a href="/wiki/Signal_processing" title="Signal processing">signal processing</a> and many other fields.</div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Plane_waves">Plane waves</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wave&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Plane waves">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Plane_wave" title="Plane wave">Plane wave</a></div>
<p>A <a href="/wiki/Plane_wave" title="Plane wave">plane wave</a> is a kind of wave whose value varies only in one spatial direction. That is, its value is constant on a plane that is perpendicular to that direction. Plane waves can be specified by a vector of unit length <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\hat {n}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</p><p>Plane waves are often used to model <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_waves" title="Electromagnetic waves">electromagnetic waves</a> far from a source. For electromagnetic plane waves, the electric and magnetic fields themselves are transverse to the direction of propagation, and also perpendicular to each other.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Standing_waves">Standing waves</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wave&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Standing waves">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Standing_wave" title="Standing wave">Standing wave</a>, <a href="/wiki/Acoustic_resonance" title="Acoustic resonance">Acoustic resonance</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Helmholtz_resonator" title="Helmholtz resonator">Helmholtz resonator</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Organ_pipe" title="Organ pipe">Organ pipe</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Standing_wave.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="250" data-file-width="750" decoding="async" height="100" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Standing_wave.gif/300px-Standing_wave.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Standing_wave.gif/450px-Standing_wave.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Standing_wave.gif/600px-Standing_wave.gif 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Standing_wave.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Standing wave. The red dots represent the wave <a href="/wiki/Node_(physics)" title="Node (physics)">nodes</a></div></div></div>
<p>A standing wave, also known as a <i>stationary wave</i>, is a wave whose <a href="/wiki/Envelope_(waves)" title="Envelope (waves)">envelope</a> remains in a constant position. This phenomenon arises as a result of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Interference_(wave_propagation)" title="Interference (wave propagation)">interference</a> between two waves traveling in opposite directions.
</p><p>The <i>sum</i> of two counter-propagating waves (of equal amplitude and frequency) creates a <i>standing wave</i>. Standing waves commonly arise when a boundary blocks further propagation of the wave, thus causing wave reflection, and therefore introducing a counter-propagating wave. For example, when a <a href="/wiki/Violin" title="Violin">violin</a> string is displaced, transverse waves propagate out to where the string is held in place at the <a href="/wiki/Bridge_(instrument)" title="Bridge (instrument)">bridge</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Nut_(string_instrument)" title="Nut (string instrument)">nut</a>, where the waves are reflected back. At the bridge and nut, the two opposed waves are in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antiphase" title="Antiphase">antiphase</a> and cancel each other, producing a <a href="/wiki/Node_(physics)" title="Node (physics)">node</a>. Halfway between two nodes there is an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antinode" title="Antinode">antinode</a>, where the two counter-propagating waves <i>enhance</i> each other maximally. There is no net <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Energy_transfer" title="Energy transfer">propagation of energy</a> over time.
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional">
