<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_properties">Physical properties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wave&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Physical properties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Light_dispersion_of_a_mercury-vapor_lamp_with_a_flint_glass_prism_IPNr%C2%B00125.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2818" data-file-width="2309" decoding="async" height="207" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Light_dispersion_of_a_mercury-vapor_lamp_with_a_flint_glass_prism_IPNr%C2%B00125.jpg/170px-Light_dispersion_of_a_mercury-vapor_lamp_with_a_flint_glass_prism_IPNr%C2%B00125.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Light_dispersion_of_a_mercury-vapor_lamp_with_a_flint_glass_prism_IPNr%C2%B00125.jpg/255px-Light_dispersion_of_a_mercury-vapor_lamp_with_a_flint_glass_prism_IPNr%C2%B00125.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Light_dispersion_of_a_mercury-vapor_lamp_with_a_flint_glass_prism_IPNr%C2%B00125.jpg/340px-Light_dispersion_of_a_mercury-vapor_lamp_with_a_flint_glass_prism_IPNr%C2%B00125.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Light_dispersion_of_a_mercury-vapor_lamp_with_a_flint_glass_prism_IPNr%C2%B00125.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Light beam exhibiting reflection, refraction, transmission and dispersion when encountering a prism</div></div></div>
<p>Waves exhibit common behaviors under a number of standard situations, for example:
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Transmission_and_media">Transmission and media</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wave&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Transmission and media">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Rectilinear_propagation" title="Rectilinear propagation">Rectilinear propagation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Transmittance" title="Transmittance">Transmittance</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Transmission_medium" title="Transmission medium">Transmission medium</a></div>
<p>Waves normally move in a straight line (that is, rectilinearly) through a <i><a href="/wiki/Transmission_medium" title="Transmission medium">transmission medium</a></i>. Such media can be classified into one or more of the following categories:
</p>
<ul><li>A <i>bounded medium</i> if it is finite in extent, otherwise an <i>unbounded medium</i></li>
<li>A <i>linear medium</i> if the amplitudes of different waves at any particular point in the medium can be added</li>
<li>A <i>uniform medium</i> or <i>homogeneous medium</i> if its physical properties are unchanged at different locations in space</li>
<li>An <i>anisotropic medium</i> if one or more of its physical properties differ in one or more directions</li>
<li>An <i>isotropic medium</i> if its physical properties are the <i>same</i> in all directions</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Absorption">Absorption</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wave&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Absorption">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Absorption_(acoustics)" title="Absorption (acoustics)">Absorption (acoustics)</a> and <a href="/wiki/Absorption_(electromagnetic_radiation)" title="Absorption (electromagnetic radiation)">Absorption (electromagnetic radiation)</a></div>
<p>Waves are usually defined in media which allow most or all of a wave's energy to propagate without <a href="/wiki/Insertion_loss" title="Insertion loss">loss</a>. However materials may be characterized as "lossy" if they remove energy from a wave, usually converting it into heat. This is termed "absorption." A material which absorbs a wave's energy, either in transmission or reflection, is characterized by a <a href="/wiki/Refractive_index" title="Refractive index">refractive index</a> which is <a href="/wiki/Complex_number" title="Complex number">complex</a>. The amount of absorption will generally depend on the frequency (wavelength) of the wave, which, for instance, explains why objects may appear colored.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reflection">Reflection</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wave&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Reflection">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Reflection_(physics)" title="Reflection (physics)">Reflection (physics)</a></div>
<p>When a wave strikes a reflective surface, it changes direction, such that the angle made by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Incident_ray" title="Incident ray">incident wave</a> and line <a href="/wiki/Perpendicular" title="Perpendicular">normal</a> to the surface equals the angle made by the reflected wave and the same normal line.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Refraction">Refraction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wave&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Refraction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Refraction" title="Refraction">Refraction</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wave_refraction.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="141" data-file-width="201" decoding="async" height="140" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Wave_refraction.gif/200px-Wave_refraction.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/49/Wave_refraction.gif 1.5x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wave_refraction.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sinusoidal traveling plane wave entering a region of lower wave velocity at an angle, illustrating the decrease in wavelength and change of direction (refraction) that results.</div></div></div>
<p>Refraction is the phenomenon of a wave changing its speed. Mathematically, this means that the size of the <a href="/wiki/Phase_velocity" title="Phase velocity">phase velocity</a> changes. Typically, refraction occurs when a wave passes from one <a href="/wiki/Transmission_medium" title="Transmission medium">medium</a> into another. The amount by which a wave is refracted by a material is given by the <a href="/wiki/Refractive_index" title="Refractive index">refractive index</a> of the material. The directions of incidence and refraction are related to the refractive indices of the two materials by <a href="/wiki/Snell%27s_law" title="Snell's law">Snell's law</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Diffraction">Diffraction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wave&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Diffraction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Diffraction" title="Diffraction">Diffraction</a></div>
<p>A wave exhibits diffraction when it encounters an obstacle that bends the wave or when it spreads after emerging from an opening. Diffraction effects are more pronounced when the size of the obstacle or opening is comparable to the wavelength of the wave.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Interference">Interference</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wave&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Interference">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Interference_(wave_propagation)" title="Interference (wave propagation)">Interference (wave propagation)</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Two_sources_interference.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="200" data-file-width="200" decoding="async" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/2c/Two_sources_interference.gif" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption">Identical waves from two sources undergoing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Interference_(wave_propagation)" title="Interference (wave propagation)">interference</a>. Observed at the bottom one sees 5 positions where the waves add in phase, but in between which they are out of phase and cancel.</div></div></div>
<p>When waves in a linear medium (the usual case) cross each other in a region of space, they do not actually interact with each other, but continue on as if the other one weren't present. However at any point <i>in</i> that region the <i>field quantities</i> describing those waves add according to the <a href="/wiki/Superposition_principle" title="Superposition principle">superposition principle</a>. If the waves are of the same frequency in a fixed <a href="/wiki/Phase_(waves)" title="Phase (waves)">phase</a> relationship, then there will generally be positions at which the two waves are <i>in phase</i> and their amplitudes <i>add</i>, and other positions where they are <i>out of phase</i> and their amplitudes (partially or fully) <i>cancel</i>. This is called an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Interference_(wave_propagation)" title="Interference (wave propagation)">interference pattern</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Polarization">Polarization</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wave&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Polarization">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Polarization_(waves)" title="Polarization (waves)">Polarization (waves)</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Circular.Polarization.Circularly.Polarized.Light_Circular.Polarizer_Creating.Left.Handed.Helix.View.svg"><img alt="Circular.Polarization.Circularly.Polarized.Light Circular.Polarizer Creating.Left.Handed.Helix.View.svg" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="348" data-file-width="791" decoding="async" height="97" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Circular.Polarization.Circularly.Polarized.Light_Circular.Polarizer_Creating.Left.Handed.Helix.View.svg/220px-Circular.Polarization.Circularly.Polarized.Light_Circular.Polarizer_Creating.Left.Handed.Helix.View.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Circular.Polarization.Circularly.Polarized.Light_Circular.Polarizer_Creating.Left.Handed.Helix.View.svg/330px-Circular.Polarization.Circularly.Polarized.Light_Circular.Polarizer_Creating.Left.Handed.Helix.View.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Circular.Polarization.Circularly.Polarized.Light_Circular.Polarizer_Creating.Left.Handed.Helix.View.svg/440px-Circular.Polarization.Circularly.Polarized.Light_Circular.Polarizer_Creating.Left.Handed.Helix.View.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Circular.Polarization.Circularly.Polarized.Light_Circular.Polarizer_Creating.Left.Handed.Helix.View.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div></div></div></div>
<p>The phenomenon of polarization arises when wave motion can occur simultaneously in two <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orthogonal" title="Orthogonal">orthogonal</a> directions. <a href="/wiki/Transverse_wave" title="Transverse wave">Transverse waves</a> can be polarized, for instance. When polarization is used as a descriptor without qualification, it usually refers to the special, simple case of <a href="/wiki/Linear_polarization" title="Linear polarization">linear polarization</a>. A transverse wave is linearly polarized if it oscillates in only one direction or plane. In the case of linear polarization, it is often useful to add the relative orientation of that plane, perpendicular to the direction of travel, in which the oscillation occurs, such as "horizontal" for instance, if the plane of polarization is parallel to the ground. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_waves" title="Electromagnetic waves">Electromagnetic waves</a> propagating in free space, for instance, are transverse; they can be polarized by the use of a <a href="/wiki/Polarizer" title="Polarizer">polarizing filter</a>.
</p><p>Longitudinal waves, such as sound waves, do not exhibit polarization. For these waves there is only one direction of oscillation, that is, along the direction of travel.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dispersion">Dispersion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wave&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Dispersion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:272px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Light_dispersion_conceptual_waves.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="640" decoding="async" height="203" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Light_dispersion_conceptual_waves.gif/270px-Light_dispersion_conceptual_waves.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Light_dispersion_conceptual_waves.gif/405px-Light_dispersion_conceptual_waves.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Light_dispersion_conceptual_waves.gif/540px-Light_dispersion_conceptual_waves.gif 2x" width="270"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Light_dispersion_conceptual_waves.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Schematic of light being dispersed by a prism. Click to see animation.</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Dispersion_relation" title="Dispersion relation">Dispersion relation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dispersion_(optics)" title="Dispersion (optics)">Dispersion (optics)</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Dispersion_(water_waves)" title="Dispersion (water waves)">Dispersion (water waves)</a></div>
<p>A wave undergoes dispersion when either the <a href="/wiki/Phase_velocity" title="Phase velocity">phase velocity</a> or the <a href="/wiki/Group_velocity" title="Group velocity">group velocity</a> depends on the wave frequency.
Dispersion is most easily seen by letting white light pass through a <a href="/wiki/Prism_(optics)" title="Prism (optics)">prism</a>, the result of which is to produce the spectrum of colors of the rainbow. <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Newton" title="Isaac Newton">Isaac Newton</a> performed experiments with light and prisms, presenting his findings in the <i><a href="/wiki/Opticks" title="Opticks">Opticks</a></i> (1704) that white light consists of several colors and that these colors cannot be decomposed any further.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Newton_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Newton-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Mechanical_waves">Mechanical waves</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wave&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Mechanical waves">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Mechanical_wave" title="Mechanical wave">Mechanical wave</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Waves_on_strings">Waves on strings</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wave&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Waves on strings">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vibrating_string" title="Vibrating string">Vibrating string</a></div>
<p>The speed of a transverse wave traveling along a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vibrating_string" title="Vibrating string">vibrating string</a> (<i>v</i>) is directly proportional to the square root of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tension_(mechanics)" title="Tension (mechanics)">tension</a> of the string (<i>T</i>) over the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Linear_mass_density" title="Linear mass density">linear mass density</a> (<i>μ</i>):
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle v={\sqrt {\frac {T}{\mu }}},}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>v</mi>
<mo>=</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<msqrt>
<mfrac>
<mi>T</mi>
<mi>μ<!-- μ --></mi>
</mfrac>
</msqrt>
</mrow>
<mo>,</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle v={\sqrt {\frac {T}{\mu }}},}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle v={\sqrt {\frac {T}{\mu }}},}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/52120bdd3abf547889106c14898e69b2acef1b13" style="vertical-align: -3.338ex; width:9.669ex; height:7.676ex;"/></span></dd></dl>
<p>where the linear density <i>μ</i> is the mass per unit length of the string.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Acoustic_waves">Acoustic waves</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wave&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Acoustic waves">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Acoustic_wave" title="Acoustic wave">Acoustic wave</a></div>
<p>Acoustic or <a href="/wiki/Sound" title="Sound">sound</a> waves travel at speed given by
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle v={\sqrt {\frac {B}{\rho _{0}}}},}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>v</mi>
<mo>=</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<msqrt>
<mfrac>
<mi>B</mi>
<msub>
<mi>ρ<!-- ρ --></mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>0</mn>
</mrow>
</msub>
</mfrac>
</msqrt>
</mrow>
<mo>,</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle v={\sqrt {\frac {B}{\rho _{0}}}},}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle v={\sqrt {\frac {B}{\rho _{0}}}},}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c754a0dd870dde6c2ca6b773849dcfb3dc23f7f6" style="vertical-align: -3.338ex; width:10.289ex; height:7.676ex;"/></span></dd></dl>
<p>or the square root of the adiabatic bulk modulus divided by the ambient fluid density (see <a href="/wiki/Speed_of_sound" title="Speed of sound">speed of sound</a>).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Water_waves">Water waves</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wave&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Water waves">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Shallow_water_wave.gif"><img alt="Shallow water wave.gif" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="70" data-file-width="466" decoding="async" height="60" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Shallow_water_wave.gif/400px-Shallow_water_wave.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/be/Shallow_water_wave.gif 1.5x" width="400"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Shallow_water_wave.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div></div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Water_waves" title="Water waves">Water waves</a></div>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Ripple_tank" title="Ripple tank">Ripples</a> on the surface of a pond are actually a combination of transverse and longitudinal waves; therefore, the points on the surface follow orbital paths.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Sound" title="Sound">Sound</a> – a mechanical wave that propagates through gases, liquids, solids and plasmas;</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inertial_waves" title="Inertial waves">Inertial waves</a>, which occur in rotating fluids and are restored by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coriolis_effect" title="Coriolis effect">Coriolis effect</a>;</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ocean_surface_wave" title="Ocean surface wave">Ocean surface waves</a>, which are perturbations that propagate through water.</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Seismic_waves">Seismic waves</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wave&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Seismic waves">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seismic_waves" title="Seismic waves">Seismic waves</a></div>
<p>Seismic waves are waves of energy that travel through the Earth's layers, and are a result of earthquakes, volcanic eruptions, magma movement, large landslides and large man-made explosions that give out low-frequency acoustic energy.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Doppler_effect">Doppler effect</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wave&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Doppler effect">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <b>Doppler effect</b> (or the <b>Doppler shift</b>) is the change in <a href="/wiki/Frequency" title="Frequency">frequency</a> of a wave in relation to an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Observer_(physics)" title="Observer (physics)">observer</a> who is moving relative to the wave source.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Giordano_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Giordano-19">[19]</a></sup> It is named after the <a href="/wiki/Austria" title="Austria">Austrian</a> physicist <a href="/wiki/Christian_Doppler" title="Christian Doppler">Christian Doppler</a>, who described the phenomenon in 1842.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Shock_waves">Shock waves</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wave&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Shock waves">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Transonico-en.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1500" data-file-width="2504" decoding="async" height="180" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Transonico-en.svg/300px-Transonico-en.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Transonico-en.svg/450px-Transonico-en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Transonico-en.svg/600px-Transonico-en.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Transonico-en.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Formation of a shock wave by a plane.</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Shock_wave" title="Shock wave">Shock wave</a></div>
<p>A shock wave is a type of propagating disturbance. When a wave moves faster than the local <a href="/wiki/Speed_of_sound" title="Speed of sound">speed of sound</a> in a <a href="/wiki/Fluid" title="Fluid">fluid</a>, it is a shock wave. Like an ordinary wave, a shock wave carries energy and can propagate through a medium; however, it is characterized by an abrupt, nearly discontinuous change in <a href="/wiki/Pressure" title="Pressure">pressure</a>, <a href="/wiki/Temperature" title="Temperature">temperature</a> and <a href="/wiki/Density" title="Density">density</a> of the medium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Sonic_boom" title="Sonic boom">Sonic boom</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cherenkov_radiation" title="Cherenkov radiation">Cherenkov radiation</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other">Other</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wave&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Other">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul><li>Waves of <a href="/wiki/Traffic_wave" title="Traffic wave">traffic</a>, that is, propagation of different densities of motor vehicles, and so forth, which can be modeled as kinematic waves<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lighthill_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lighthill-21">[21]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Metachronal_rhythm" title="Metachronal rhythm">Metachronal wave</a> refers to the appearance of a traveling wave produced by coordinated sequential actions.</li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Electromagnetic_waves">Electromagnetic waves</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wave&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Electromagnetic waves">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Onde_electromagn%C3%A9tique.png"><img alt="Onde electromagnétique.png" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1073" data-file-width="1621" decoding="async" height="199" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Onde_electromagn%C3%A9tique.png/300px-Onde_electromagn%C3%A9tique.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Onde_electromagn%C3%A9tique.png/450px-Onde_electromagn%C3%A9tique.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Onde_electromagn%C3%A9tique.png/600px-Onde_electromagn%C3%A9tique.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Onde_electromagn%C3%A9tique.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div></div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_wave" title="Electromagnetic wave">Electromagnetic wave</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_spectrum" title="Electromagnetic spectrum">Electromagnetic spectrum</a></div>
<p>An electromagnetic wave consists of two waves that are oscillations of the <a href="/wiki/Electric_field" title="Electric field">electric</a> and <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_field" title="Magnetic field">magnetic</a> fields. An electromagnetic wave travels in a direction that is at right angles to the oscillation direction of both fields. In the 19th century, <a href="/wiki/James_Clerk_Maxwell" title="James Clerk Maxwell">James Clerk Maxwell</a> showed that, in <a href="/wiki/Vacuum" title="Vacuum">vacuum</a>, the electric and magnetic fields satisfy the <a href="/wiki/Wave_equation" title="Wave equation">wave equation</a> both with speed equal to that of the <a href="/wiki/Speed_of_light" title="Speed of light">speed of light</a>. From this emerged the idea that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Visible_light" title="Visible light">light</a> is an electromagnetic wave. Electromagnetic waves can have different frequencies (and thus wavelengths), giving rise to various types of radiation such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radio_waves" title="Radio waves">radio waves</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Microwaves" title="Microwaves">microwaves</a>, <a href="/wiki/Infrared" title="Infrared">infrared</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Visible_light" title="Visible light">visible light</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">ultraviolet</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/X-rays" title="X-rays">X-rays</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gamma_rays" title="Gamma rays">Gamma rays</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Quantum_mechanical_waves">Quantum mechanical waves</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wave&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Quantum mechanical waves">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Schr%C3%B6dinger_equation" title="Schrödinger equation">Schrödinger equation</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Wave_function" title="Wave function">Wave function</a></div>
<h3><span id="Schr.C3.B6dinger_equation"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Schrödinger_equation">Schrödinger equation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wave&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Schrödinger equation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Schr%C3%B6dinger_equation" title="Schrödinger equation">Schrödinger equation</a> describes the wave-like behavior of particles in <a href="/wiki/Quantum_mechanics" title="Quantum mechanics">quantum mechanics</a>. Solutions of this equation are <a href="/wiki/Wave_function" title="Wave function">wave functions</a> which can be used to describe the probability density of a particle.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dirac_equation">Dirac equation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wave&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Dirac equation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Dirac_equation" title="Dirac equation">Dirac equation</a> is a relativistic wave equation detailing electromagnetic interactions. Dirac waves accounted for the fine details of the hydrogen spectrum in a completely rigorous way. The wave equation also implied the existence of a new form of matter, antimatter, previously unsuspected and unobserved and which was experimentally confirmed. In the context of quantum field theory, the Dirac equation is reinterpreted to describe quantum fields corresponding to spin-½ particles.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wave_packet_(dispersion).gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="115" data-file-width="200" decoding="async" height="115" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/b0/Wave_packet_%28dispersion%29.gif" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wave_packet_(dispersion).gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A propagating wave packet; in general, the <i>envelope</i> of the wave packet moves at a different speed than the constituent waves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fromhold_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fromhold-22">[22]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="de_Broglie_waves">de Broglie waves</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wave&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: de Broglie waves">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Wave_packet" title="Wave packet">Wave packet</a> and <a href="/wiki/Matter_wave" title="Matter wave">Matter wave</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Louis_de_Broglie" title="Louis de Broglie">Louis de Broglie</a> postulated that all particles with <a href="/wiki/Momentum" title="Momentum">momentum</a> have a wavelength
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \lambda ={\frac {h}{p}},}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>λ<!-- λ --></mi>
<mo>=</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mfrac>
<mi>h</mi>
<mi>p</mi>
</mfrac>
</mrow>
<mo>,</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \lambda ={\frac {h}{p}},}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\lambda ={\frac {h}{p}}," aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/ea61dba4f570d474341b6f5ff22b0f26f52c1a87" style="vertical-align: -2.338ex; width:7.276ex; height:5.843ex;"/></span></dd></dl>
<p>where <i>h</i> is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Planck%27s_constant" title="Planck's constant">Planck's constant</a>, and <i>p</i> is the magnitude of the <a href="/wiki/Momentum" title="Momentum">momentum</a> of the particle. This hypothesis was at the basis of <a href="/wiki/Quantum_mechanics" title="Quantum mechanics">quantum mechanics</a>. Nowadays, this wavelength is called the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/De_Broglie_wavelength" title="De Broglie wavelength">de Broglie wavelength</a>. For example, the <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">electrons</a> in a <a href="/wiki/Cathode-ray_tube" title="Cathode-ray tube">CRT</a> display have a de Broglie wavelength of about 10<sup>−13</sup> m.
</p><p>A wave representing such a particle traveling in the <i>k</i>-direction is expressed by the wave function as follows:
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \psi (\mathbf {r} ,\,t=0)=Ae^{i\mathbf {k\cdot r} },}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

<p>where the wavelength is determined by the <a href="/wiki/Wave_vector" title="Wave vector">wave vector</a> <b>k</b> as:
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \lambda ={\frac {2\pi }{k}},}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</mstyle>

</semantics>

<p>and the momentum by:
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \mathbf {p} =\hbar \mathbf {k} .}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</mrow>
<mo>.</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \mathbf {p} =\hbar \mathbf {k} .}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle \mathbf {p} =\hbar \mathbf {k} .}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/ad88d71b7b8f4e1a519044f8b61f53b820c8da24" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:7.948ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span></dd></dl>
<p>However, a wave like this with definite wavelength is not localized in space, and so cannot represent a particle localized in space. To localize a particle, de Broglie proposed a superposition of different wavelengths ranging around a central value in a <a href="/wiki/Wave_packet" title="Wave packet">wave packet</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marton_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Marton-23">[23]</a></sup> a waveform often used in <a href="/wiki/Quantum_mechanics" title="Quantum mechanics">quantum mechanics</a> to describe the <a href="/wiki/Wave_function" title="Wave function">wave function</a> of a particle. In a wave packet, the wavelength of the particle is not precise, and the local wavelength deviates on either side of the main wavelength value.
</p><p>In representing the wave function of a localized particle, the <a href="/wiki/Wave_packet" title="Wave packet">wave packet</a> is often taken to have a <a href="/wiki/Gaussian_function" title="Gaussian function">Gaussian shape</a> and is called a <i>Gaussian wave packet</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wavepacket_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-wavepacket-24">[24]</a></sup> Gaussian wave packets also are used to analyze water waves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mei_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mei-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p><p>For example, a Gaussian wavefunction <i>ψ</i> might take the form:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bromley_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bromley-26">[26]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \psi (x,\,t=0)=A\exp \left(-{\frac {x^{2}}{2\sigma ^{2}}}+ik_{0}x\right),}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle \psi (x,\,t=0)=A\exp \left(-{\frac {x^{2}}{2\sigma ^{2}}}+ik_{0}x\right),}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/fe8f59590d2409afa58f4d47cf3d654049d02292" style="vertical-align: -2.505ex; width:37.452ex; height:6.343ex;"/></span></dd></dl>
<p>at some initial time <i>t</i> = 0, where the central wavelength is related to the central wave vector <i>k</i><sub>0</sub> as λ<sub>0</sub> = 2π / <i>k</i><sub>0</sub>. It is well known from the theory of <a href="/wiki/Fourier_analysis" title="Fourier analysis">Fourier analysis</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brandt_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brandt-27">[27]</a></sup> or from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heisenberg_uncertainty_principle" title="Heisenberg uncertainty principle">Heisenberg uncertainty principle</a> (in the case of quantum mechanics) that a narrow range of wavelengths is necessary to produce a localized wave packet, and the more localized the envelope, the larger the spread in required wavelengths. </p>

</p>

<p>that is, a number of waves of wavelengths λ such that <i>k</i>λ = 2 π.
</p><p>The parameter σ decides the spatial spread of the Gaussian along the <i>x</i>-axis, while the Fourier transform shows a spread in <a href="/wiki/Wave_vector" title="Wave vector">wave vector</a> <i>k</i> determined by 1/<i>σ</i>. That is, the smaller the extent in space, the larger the extent in <i>k</i>, and hence in λ = 2π/<i>k</i>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:GravitationalWave_CrossPolarization.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="288" data-file-width="288" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/GravitationalWave_CrossPolarization.gif/220px-GravitationalWave_CrossPolarization.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/b8/GravitationalWave_CrossPolarization.gif 1.5x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:GravitationalWave_CrossPolarization.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Animation showing the effect of a cross-polarized gravitational wave on a ring of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Test_particles" title="Test particles">test particles</a></div></div></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Gravity_waves">Gravity waves</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wave&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Gravity waves">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gravity_waves" title="Gravity waves">Gravity waves</a> are waves generated in a fluid medium or at the interface between two media when the force of gravity or buoyancy tries to restore equilibrium. A ripple on a pond is one example.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Gravitational_waves">Gravitational waves</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wave&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Gravitational waves">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_wave" title="Gravitational wave">Gravitational wave</a></div>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gravitational_radiation" title="Gravitational radiation">Gravitational waves</a> also travel through space. The first observation of gravitational waves was announced on 11 February 2016.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup>
Gravitational waves are disturbances in the curvature of <a href="/wiki/Spacetime" title="Spacetime">spacetime</a>, predicted by Einstein's theory of <a href="/wiki/General_relativity" title="General relativity">general relativity</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wave&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Index_of_wave_articles" title="Index of wave articles">Index of wave articles</a></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Waves_in_general">Waves in general</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wave&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Waves in general">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r998391716">.mw-parser-output .div-col{margin-top:0.3em;column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .div-col-small{font-size:90%}.mw-parser-output .div-col-rules{column-rule:1px solid #aaa}.mw-parser-output .div-col dl,.mw-parser-output .div-col ol,.mw-parser-output .div-col ul{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .div-col li,.mw-parser-output .div-col dd{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}</style><div class="div-col" style="column-width: 30em;">
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Wave_equation" title="Wave equation">Wave equation</a>, general</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/One-way_wave_equation" title="One-way wave equation">One-way wave equation</a>, for waves running in pre-defined direction</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Wave_propagation" title="Wave propagation">Wave propagation</a>, any of the ways in which waves travel</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Interference_(wave_propagation)" title="Interference (wave propagation)">Interference (wave propagation)</a>, a phenomenon in which two waves superpose to form a resultant wave</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Mechanical_wave" title="Mechanical wave">Mechanical wave</a>, in media transmission</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Wave_Motion_(journal)" title="Wave Motion (journal)"><i>Wave Motion</i> (journal)</a>, a scientific journal</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Wavefront" title="Wavefront">Wavefront</a>, an advancing surface of wave propagation</li></ul></div>
